  <span id="freeText11049444026785339716" style="display:none">Mph. This was well written and I was really enjoying it. Lovely sense of time, stroppy unlikeable heroine, interesting steampunky 'science' without too much annoying airship bobbins and a great concept. But I do wish there had been a proper plot--it just kind of evaporated towards the end, leaving me with a sense that there's less to this than meets the eye. <br /><br />Also, there are multiple problematic issues with the Japanese elements (and also just stupid ones. A sequence where someone who doesn't speak or read Japanese can decipher a letter in a reasonably short amount of time simply by using a kanji-English dictionary? Are you kidding me?!), and then it turns out the author is a Daiwa scholar who lived in Japan for 18 months or something, which surprised me. <br /><br />Mph, again. I wanted to like it more than I did, the author can write and has ideas, but ultimately insubstantial.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1380469122" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1380469122" />
    <a title="Olga Godim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim"><img alt="Olga Godim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442639299p2/7438487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1380469122?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olga Godim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olga Godim" href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim">Olga Godim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7438487-olga-godim?shelf=mainstream">mainstream</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7438487-olga-godim?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1380469122" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18312651383444948736"><b>4.5 stars</b><br/>It is hard to pigeonhole this book. Literary? I’m not sure. Historical? Kind-of. It does deal with a historical period. Steampunk? Not really, even though the characters have some paranormal abilities, and the Victorian era adds credibility to such a label. The closest I can come up with is magic realism. <br/>Magic shimmers on the pages of this book. It defines the story and the protagonists. And the attraction of this book for me was definitely magical, as I don’t usually like literary or </span>
  <span id="freeText18312651383444948736" style="display:none"><b>4.5 stars</b><br />It is hard to pigeonhole this book. Literary? I’m not sure. Historical? Kind-of. It does deal with a historical period. Steampunk? Not really, even though the characters have some paranormal abilities, and the Victorian era adds credibility to such a label. The closest I can come up with is magic realism. <br />Magic shimmers on the pages of this book. It defines the story and the protagonists. And the attraction of this book for me was definitely magical, as I don’t usually like literary or historical or steampunk. <br />I won’t go into the plot rehashing here – it’s complicated, and I don’t want to spoil the fun of reading for the others. I’ll concentrate on the characters instead. <br />The hero of this book is Keita Mori, a Japanese watchmaker living in London in 1883. Or is he a Japanese nobleman? A spy? A philosopher? A magician? A mad genius? All of the above? Here is one of his short conversations that snapped my attention, demonstrating his sideway mode of thinking. He talks about one of his clockwork toys <blockquote>
  <b>‘It’s a steam engine toy,’ Mori said. ‘An old design. The Ancient Greeks had them.’<br />‘The Ancient Greeks? If they had steam engines, why didn’t they have trains?’<br />Mori twitched his shoulder. ‘They were philosophers. They put two and two together and got a goldfish.’ </b>
</blockquote> The reader is facing a multitude of questions about Mori, and none of them is easy to answer. Mori is as much an enigma as the book about him, even after the last page is turned. His extremely complex psyche enchants some players of the story, baffles the others, and invoke hatred in the third category. He is powerful but vulnerable, suffering but loving, kind to his friends and ruthless to his enemies. He is almost alive, despite his mysterious paranormal gift. Or is it a curse?<br />The author made Mori the protagonist of her book but she didn’t give him a voice. Nothing is told from his POV. Everything we know about him comes through the eyes and words of others, the ones pulled into his sphere by the strange events of the novel and the magnetism of his personality: his friend Thaniel; his former employer, a Japanese politician Ito; and a young woman Grace, a student of physics at Oxford and a daughter of a lord. Mori is a catalyst of their lives, almost an instigator of their fates, but he doesn’t benefit from his unusual talent himself.<br />Thaniel, another hero of the story, is a clerk in Whitehall. His encounter with Mori overturns his boring existence, reshapes his future, makes him ‘more,’ inspires him to grow. Thaniel could’ve been a hero of his own story, he has the potential, but he fades beside Mori, becomes a cog in the Watchmaker’s elaborate schemes, even though they make him more significant as a human being. <br />Grace, realizing what’s happening to Thaniel, tries to interfere, to put him on the path of his own choosing, but her actions take a cruel turn. In a way, she is as ingenious and ruthless as Mori himself, but unlike him, she is acting out of self-interest, not love, like Mori, and in the end, she becomes the villain of the book. <br />Besides multifaceted interpersonal dynamics, many other lines intertwine in this tale – politics and Irish nationalists, bombs and rabid racism, the arrogance of the upper classes and the sacrifices of poor people, music and mechanical inventions – making the narration as true as a kaleidoscope. Making the readers care. Making them want to understand.<br />Recommended to anyone. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18312651383444948736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1334001057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14864013897104034191">Every now and again a book turns up that appears to be everywhere in Goodreads and the blog world. In the past month or so, this was one such book. When I saw it come up on a special offer I thought sure lets give it a go. <br/><br/>Honestly I have no idea why this book has been so popular recently or is getting rated so highly. It started off interesting enough, though honestly from the beginning I never felt hooked. It went down hill from there. My impressions was the author had a somewhat good idea but</span>
  <span id="freeText14864013897104034191" style="display:none">Every now and again a book turns up that appears to be everywhere in Goodreads and the blog world. In the past month or so, this was one such book. When I saw it come up on a special offer I thought sure lets give it a go. <br /><br />Honestly I have no idea why this book has been so popular recently or is getting rated so highly. It started off interesting enough, though honestly from the beginning I never felt hooked. It went down hill from there. My impressions was the author had a somewhat good idea but then got lost half way through. It all became a bit farcical and unbelievable. Part of the problem may be that it seems to fall between two stools in terms of genre. Its not historical fiction (though seems to start out a such) and its not fantasy (though contains clear elements of such). However neither element is strong enough to carry the story. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8453783126144915321">This was a nice meandering tale but when I finished the book it all felt a little pointless. We follow Thanial, a working class man in London in a fictional 1880s setting. He finds a watch, and soon meets the man who created it, a Japanese watchmaker living in South Kensington. They strike up an odd friendship.<br/><br/>Separately we meet Grace, an Oxford educated physicist who doesn't want to meet a man and settle down, all she wants is to continue a career in a time where women weren't encouraged to pra</span>
  <span id="freeText8453783126144915321" style="display:none">This was a nice meandering tale but when I finished the book it all felt a little pointless. We follow Thanial, a working class man in London in a fictional 1880s setting. He finds a watch, and soon meets the man who created it, a Japanese watchmaker living in South Kensington. They strike up an odd friendship.<br /><br />Separately we meet Grace, an Oxford educated physicist who doesn't want to meet a man and settle down, all she wants is to continue a career in a time where women weren't encouraged to practice science. I got the feeling the author didn't want us to like this character but I preferred her to the other two leads. At least she had some agency.<br /><br />I enjoyed the book ok but had no drive to read it and I felt the plot was pretty pointless.<br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1319545751" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1319545751" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2050107-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509633087p2/2050107.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1319545751?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/2050107-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1319545751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2650542852881069591">I was intrigued by the title; I was intrigued by the synopsis;I was smitten with the design of the whole thing.<br/><br/>Could a debut novel live up to all of that?<br/><br/>Yes, it could!<br/><br/>The story opens in London, and in a world that mixes the real and the fantastical in the loveliest of ways.<br/><br/>Nathaniel Steepleton – Thaniel – was a young telegraph operator at the Home Office. His life was a dull routine; he had wanted to be a pianist, but he took job so that he could help to support his widowed sister and her young</span>
  <span id="freeText2650542852881069591" style="display:none">I was intrigued by the title; I was intrigued by the synopsis;I was smitten with the design of the whole thing.<br /><br />Could a debut novel live up to all of that?<br /><br />Yes, it could!<br /><br />The story opens in London, and in a world that mixes the real and the fantastical in the loveliest of ways.<br /><br />Nathaniel Steepleton – Thaniel – was a young telegraph operator at the Home Office. His life was a dull routine; he had wanted to be a pianist, but he took job so that he could help to support his widowed sister and her young children.<br /><br />Grace Carrow, was an Oxford educated physicist, but her career was in jeopardy, because her father would not give her an inheritance unless she was married and she could not find a man to marry. Not ever her best friend, an aristocratic Japanese student would help her.<br /><br />Two very different people, two very different stories, but there was a link.<br /><br />Both Thaniel and Grace found themselves, quite inexplicably, in possession of watches that were both extraordinarily beautiful and unusually, exceptionally, functional.<br /><br />Those watches were made by The Watchmaker of Filigree Street. Keita Mori was kinsman to a Samurai lord and he had been assistant to the interior minister of Japan. But he had travelled to London to pursue his vocation, to make the finest of watches.<br /><br />Or had he?<br /><br />Scotland Yard suspected that Mori’s mechanisms had been used in a series of bombs set by Fenians. What else would explain why Thaniel’s watch had opened for the first time when he was just feet from a bomb, and sound an alarm before, moments later, the bomb exploded? That bomb saved the telegraph operator’s life, and it led him to the watchmaker.<br /><br />But Thaniel knew that the police were wrong. He was certain that the charming gentleman who created Katsu, the randomly programed, clockwork, sock-stealing octopus, who became his friend, wouldn’t use his wonderful skills to make bombs.<br /><br />Grace and Thaniel had met by then, they had recognised something in each other, they had realised that they might help each other, and a relationship had blossomed.<br /><br />But Grace agreed with the police.<br /><br />What other explanation could there be …. ?<br /><br />This story is beautifully and intricately crafted, and it’s clear that attention has been paid to every little detail of character, setting and plot. It rewards slow careful reading, because all of those details are important, they work together, and they draw you right into this finely wrought world.<br /><br />The plot so cleverly constructed. Sometimes I could see where it was going, sometimes I couldn’t, but in the end it all made wonderful sense.<br /><br />This is a book that asks questions about life; about how predictable, how predetermined, how comprehensible it might or might not be. You might chose to consider those questions, or you might want to simply enjoy the journey through this lovely book.<br /><br />I’m sure that there’s much more that could be said, but I’m still caught up and enjoying the wonder of it all.<br /><br />I could see the influence of other books and authors, but Natasha Pulley has taken those influences and built on them to create something quite distinctive of her own.<br /><br />This isn’t the kind of book I read often, but I have to say that I loved stepping into her world, and I would gladly step into it again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2650542852881069591" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1323963331" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1323963331" />
    <a title="Althea Ann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann"><img alt="Althea Ann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411566466p2/3846743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1323963331?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Althea Ann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Althea Ann" href="/user/show/3846743-althea-ann">Althea Ann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1323963331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14858045804505223962">Thaniel Steepleton is an unassuming British public servant. Once, he had dreams of becoming a pianist, but now he has to support his distant, widowed sister's family, so he's tied to his job as a telegraph operator, and doesn't expect anything in his rather dull and ascetic life to change.<br/><br/>However, an anonymous gift of a clearly valuable watch becomes suspicious when it ends up saving him from an Irish terrorist group's bomb. Soon, he's assigned to spy on Keita Mori, a Japanese watchmaker living </span>
  <span id="freeText14858045804505223962" style="display:none">Thaniel Steepleton is an unassuming British public servant. Once, he had dreams of becoming a pianist, but now he has to support his distant, widowed sister's family, so he's tied to his job as a telegraph operator, and doesn't expect anything in his rather dull and ascetic life to change.<br /><br />However, an anonymous gift of a clearly valuable watch becomes suspicious when it ends up saving him from an Irish terrorist group's bomb. Soon, he's assigned to spy on Keita Mori, a Japanese watchmaker living in London... a job where his sympathies will end up being sorely tested.<br /><br />Meanwhile, a young student, Grace Carrow, is conducting experiments designed to prove the existence of ether. However, her scientific career seems fated to come to an abrupt end due to her gender: her father will not give her an inheritance unless she is married, and so far her inquiries have not been fruitful. Her best friend, an aristocratic Japanese student, seems thoroughly uninterested in the possibility of marriage to an Englishwoman, so her future is uncertain.<br /><br />When it comes right down to it, this is a faux-Victorian paranormal romance. However, it's told with a delicate touch. The style reminded me of the 'filigree' of the title - ornately detailed, finely crafted, and lovely. The magical elements are understated; the romance is a slow burn where more is implied than told. Yet the story is both accessible and interest-gripping: I found myself putting down other books in order to keep on with this one instead. <br /><br />Many thanks to Bloomsbury USA and NetGalley for the opportunity to read this book - which turned out to be even better than I'd expected! As always, my opinions are my own.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14858045804505223962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1811998335" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1811998335" />
    <a title="Krystal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18989854-krystal"><img alt="Krystal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491478087p2/18989854.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1811998335?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krystal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krystal" href="/user/show/18989854-krystal">Krystal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18989854-krystal?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18989854-krystal?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18989854-krystal?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18989854-krystal?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1811998335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14047514321419369562">Okay, just give me a minute to get my thoughts in order. What a whirlwind ending!<br/><br/>Can I start by talking about the cover? Covers so rarely rate with me but this one is simply stunning. I'll admit - it's the sole reason I picked this book up in the first place. Beautiful design.<br/><br/>So.<br/><br/>It's a bit of a random story but I really enjoyed it! There's this fellow, Thaniel, who is stuck in a bit of a dead end job. He's a nice enough guy but is very routine and the highlight of his life is drinking tea. Then </span>
  <span id="freeText14047514321419369562" style="display:none">Okay, just give me a minute to get my thoughts in order. What a whirlwind ending!<br /><br />Can I start by talking about the cover? Covers so rarely rate with me but this one is simply stunning. I'll admit - it's the sole reason I picked this book up in the first place. Beautiful design.<br /><br />So.<br /><br />It's a bit of a random story but I really enjoyed it! There's this fellow, Thaniel, who is stuck in a bit of a dead end job. He's a nice enough guy but is very routine and the highlight of his life is drinking tea. Then he gets this mysterious watch that saves his life. Cue the end of normality and boring. (Not the tea, though. There is <i>so much tea.</i>)<br /><br />The watchmaker of the title is Mori, a reclusive Japanese fellow with <b>a pet clockwork octopus.</b> Katsu is probably my favourite character in the whole book, because he's this adorable lil random kleptomaniac with a thing for socks. He makes me want my own clockwork octopus. Mori is okay.<br /><br />There's also Grace, a mad scientist who does very 'un-ladylike' things and her bff Matsumoto who's a smug Japanese guy who is quite obviously in love with Grace. Heaven forbid either of them admit it, though. *eyeroll*<br /><br />Honestly I think it's the characters that make this such a delightful read. They're so different and quirky and they do things you just don't expect. I was constantly surprised by the directions this book took. It was its own, random, clockwork octopus. (Hashtag teamkatsu)<br /><br />The writing is clear and concise yet beautifully captures scenes so that the reader can easily partake in the action. I just really enjoyed the words and the language, and it was never overloaded with style gimmicks. It flowed gracefully, and every sentence owned its place.<br /><br />Overall, there was no set direction for the story so it was really a case of settling back and just enjoying the ride, which I did immensely. There's kind of the mystery of who set off the bomb, but honestly I was much more interested in what kind of mischief everyone was getting up to. The clockwork was like a dozen characters on its own.<br /><br /><u>You are now entering spoiler territory.</u><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ I absolutely was not expecting this to be a love story, so when it happened it took me completely by surprise. I was left wondering what had happened and what I'd missed and yet it just fit beautifully. It made a lot of sense and I think it was the not expecting it that made it come together so wonderfully.<br /><br />I never really got a handle on the whole 'remembering the future' thing (way too confusing for me to try to understand scientifically) so again it was just a case of going with the flow. I'd hate to stop and try examine the concept because I feel like a few plot holes might appear.<br /><br />I'm incredibly heartbroken over the loss of Katsu, and I can only hope he turns up unexpectedly one day after crawling out of the rubble. Or Mori makes a new one that is an exact replica of the old.<br /><br />I was a bit confused by the allusion of the ending - the whole, Mori setting up Matsumoto and Grace bit. Mat was on a train to Berlin, right? And he was apologising for being late? So did Mori make him late or is it thanks to Mori he was there at all? I mean I kind of just assume the latter is correct but I have no idea how that actually works. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />It was such a delightful, unusual story with such lovable <s>clockwork octopi</s> characters that really brought the story to life. Being set in 1800s England meant there was a lot of consideration of historical traditions, values and practices and it really enhanced the dynamic between the characters.<br /><br />I'm a bit scattered again I think but the takeaway message is that it's well worth a read! It's a unique piece of work unlike any novel I've ever read and I look forward to reading more from this author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14047514321419369562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1703022116" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1703022116" />
    <a title="Veronique" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12371602-veronique"><img alt="Veronique" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590509341p2/12371602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1703022116?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Veronique" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Veronique" href="/user/show/12371602-veronique">Veronique</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781620408346" href="/book/show/25663573-the-watchmaker-of-filigree-street">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=r2016">r2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12371602-veronique?shelf=stars-4-0">stars-4-0</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1703022116" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9647084086399787888">London mid 1880s, clockwork automata, possible clairvoyance and Japanese culture - what’s not to love? Pulley’s debut uses the typical elements of steampunk mixed with original aspects in an entertaining tale.<br/><br/>The narration although slow at the beginning grabbed me due to the characters, which the author made complex and intriguing. We’re at first introduced to Thaniel Steepleton, bored synesthete (sounds are colours to him) telegraphist at the Home Office, who one day finds an intricate clock de</span>
  <span id="freeText9647084086399787888" style="display:none">London mid 1880s, clockwork automata, possible clairvoyance and Japanese culture - what’s not to love? Pulley’s debut uses the typical elements of steampunk mixed with original aspects in an entertaining tale.<br /><br />The narration although slow at the beginning grabbed me due to the characters, which the author made complex and intriguing. We’re at first introduced to Thaniel Steepleton, bored synesthete (sounds are colours to him) telegraphist at the Home Office, who one day finds an intricate clock deposited anonymously in his lodgings, then to Keita Mori, the mysterious, kind, and brilliant Japanese watchmaker extraordinaire whose creations defy nature and physics, and finally to Grace Carro, a physics student in Oxford fighting to be let free with her Ether experiments. Slowly, all these protagonists come together in the midst of events, each more perplexing. Who is responsible for the Fenian bombings? Can someone really tell the future, and how would this affect the present? How would clairvoyance really work?<br /><br />The plot itself becomes something not unlike one of Mori’s inventions, spinning and rearranging itself into confusing and surprising patterns. Yet, it is not 'perfect' - some parts could have been more developed. Well, I wanted more. Ultimately this novel really deals with what makes people tick rather than action, while touching on some big questions/concepts/issues - freewill, fate, trust, gender roles, Japanese culture, and even some shades of LGBT.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9647084086399787888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RCbUOdWIo0AhIv/T3tunRDxJo7mtd5BprP1uB74RMs5M9oDlkcFUlE10UihRQKpb1JipnfnJPAsCVXHB2tYCng==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }
